## Transcript

- [00:00] Let me tell you about Oliver Sacks, the famous physician, professor and author of unusual
- [00:05] neurological case studies.
- [00:07] Well be looking at some of his fascinating research in future lessons, but for now, I
- [00:10] just want to talk about Sacks himself.
- [00:12] Although he possesses a brilliant and inquisitive mind, Dr. Sacks cannot do a simple thing that
- [00:17] your average toddler can.
- [00:19] He cant recognize his own face in the mirror.
- [00:22] Sacks has a form of prosopagnosia, a neurological disorder that impairs a persons ability
- [00:27] to perceive or recognize faces, also known as face blindness.
- [00:31] Last week we talked about how brain function is localized, and this is another peculiarly
- [00:35] excellent example of that.
- [00:37] Sacks can recognize his coffee cup on the shelf, but he cant pick out his oldest
- [00:42] friend from a crowd, because the specific sliver of his brain responsible for facial
- [00:46] recognition is malfunctioning.
- [00:47] Theres nothing wrong with his vision.
- [00:49] The sense is intact.
- [00:50] The problem is with his perception, at least when it comes to recognizing faces.
- [00:54] Prosopagnosia is a good example of how sensing and perceiving are connected, but different.
- [00:59] Sensation is the bottom-up process by which our senses, like vision, hearing and smell,
- [01:04] receive and relay outside stimuli.
- [01:06] Perception, on the other hand, is the top-down way our brains organize and interpret that
- [01:11] information and put it into context.
- [01:13] So right now at this very moment, youre probably receiving light from your screen
- [01:17] through your eyes, which will send the data of that sensation to your brain.
- [01:21] Perception meanwhile is your brain telling you that what youre seeing is a diagram
- [01:24] explaining the difference between sensation and perception, which is pretty meta.
- [01:28] Now your brain is interpreting that light as a talking person, whom your brain might
- [01:32] additionally recognize as Hank.
- [01:39] [Intro]
- [01:44] We are constantly bombarded by stimuli even though were only aware of what our own
- [01:48] senses can pick up.
- [01:49] Like I can see and hear and feel and even smell this Corgi, but I cant hunt using
- [01:54] sonar like a bat or hear a mole tunneling underground like an owl or see ultraviolet
- [01:59] and infrared light like a mantis shrimp.
- [02:02] I probably cant even smell half of what you can smell.
- [02:05] No!
- [02:06] No!
- [02:07] We have different senses.
- [02:08] Mwah mwah mwah mwah mwah.
- [02:10] Yeah.
- [02:11] Theres a lot to sense in the world, and not everybody needs to sense all the same
- [02:15] stuff.
- [02:16] So every animal has its limitations which we can talk about more precisely if we define
- [02:20] the Absolute Threshold of Sensation, the minimum stimulation needed to register a particular
- [02:25] stimulus, 50% of the time.
- [02:27] So if I play a tiny little beep in your ear and you tell me that you hear it fifty percent
- [02:30] of the times that I play it, thats your absolute threshold of sensation.
- [02:34] We have to use a percentage because sometimes I'll play the beep and youll hear it and
- [02:38] sometimes you wont even though its the exact same volume.
- [02:40] Why?
- [02:41] Because brains are complicated.
- [02:43] Detecting a weak sensory signal like that beep in daily life isnt only about the
- [02:46] strength of the stimulus.
- [02:48] Its also about your psychological state; your alertness and expectations in the moment.
- [02:53] This has to do with Signal Detection Theory, a model for predicting how and when a person
- [02:58] will detect a weak stimuli, partly based on context.
- [03:01] Exhausted new parents might hear their babys tiniest whimper, but not even register the
- [03:06] bellow of a passing train.
- [03:08] Their paranoid parent brains are so trained on their baby, it gives their senses a sort
- [03:12] of boosted ability, but only in relation to the subject of their attention.
- [03:16] Conversely, if youre experiencing constant stimulation, your senses will adjust in a
- [03:20] process called sensory adaptation.
- [03:22] It is the reason that I have to check and see if my wallet is there if its in my
- [03:26] right pocket, but if I move it to my left pocket, it feels like a big uncomfortable
- [03:29] lump.
- [03:30] Its also useful to be able to talk about our ability to detect the difference between
- [03:33] two stimuli.
- [03:34] I might go out at night and look up at the sky and, well, I know with my objective science
- [03:38] brain that no two stars have the exact same brightness, and yeah, I can tell with my eyeballs
- [03:43] that some stars are brighter than others, but other stars just look exactly the same
- [03:49] to me.
- [03:50] I cant tell the difference in their brightness.
- [03:51] Are you done?
- [03:52] Is it time for your to go?
- [03:53] Gimme, gimme a kiiiissss.
- [03:55] Yes, yes.
- [03:56] Okay.
- [03:57] Good girl.
- [03:58] The point at which one can tell the difference is the difference threshold, but its not
- [04:02] linear.
- [04:03] Like.
- [04:04] if a tiny star is just a tiny bit brighter than another tiny star, I can tell.
- [04:07] But if a big star is that same tiny amount brighter than another big star, I wont
- [04:11] be able to tell the difference.
- [04:12] This is important enough that we gave the guy who discovered it a law.
- [04:15] Webers Law says that we perceive differences on a logarithmic, not a linear scale.
- [04:20] Its not the amount of change.
- [04:22] Its the percentage change that matters.
- [04:24] Alright.
- [04:25] How about now we take a more in depth look at how one of our most powerful senses works?
- [04:30] Vision.
- [04:31] Your ability to see your face in the mirror is the result of a long but lightning quick
- [04:35] sequence of events.
- [04:37] Light bounces off your face and then off the mirror and then into your eyes, which take
- [04:41] in all that varied energy and transforms it into neural messages that your brain processes
- [04:46] and organizes into what you actually see, which is your face.
- [04:50] Or if youre looking elsewhere, you could see a coffee cup or a Corgi or a scary clown
- [04:53] holding a tiny cream pie.
- [04:55] So how do we transform light waves into meaningful information?
- [04:57] Well, lets start with the light itself.
- [04:59] What we humans see as light is only a small fraction of the full spectrum of electromagnetic
- [05:04] radiation that ranges from gamma to radio waves.
- [05:07] Now light has all kinds of fascinating characteristics that determine how we sense it, but for the
- [05:11] purposes of this topic, well understand light as traveling in waves.
- [05:15] The waves wavelength and frequency determines their hue, and their amplitude determines
- [05:20] their intensity or brightness.
- [05:21] For instance a short wave has a high frequency.
- [05:24] Our eyes register short wavelengths with high frequencies as blueish colors while we see
- [05:29] long, low frequency wavelengths as reddish hues.
- [05:31] The way we register the brightness of a color, the contrast between the orange of a sherbet
- [05:35] and the orange of a construction cone has to do with the intensity or amount of energy
- [05:40] in a given light wave.
- [05:41] Which as weve just said is determined by its amplitude.
- [05:44] Greater amplitude means higher intensity, means brighter color.
- [05:48] Someones just told me that sherbet doesnt- isnt a word that exists.
- [05:52] His name is Michael Aranda and hes a dumbhead.
- [05:55] Did you type it into the dictionary?
- [05:57] Type it into Google.
- [05:59] Ask Google about sherbet.
- [06:01] So sherbet is a thing.
- [06:02] So after taking this light in through the cornea and the pupil, it hits the transparent
- [06:06] disc behind the pupil: the lens, which focuses the light rays into specific images, and just
- [06:12] as youd expect the lens to do, it projects these images onto the retina, the inner surface
- [06:16] of the eyeball that contains all the receptor cells that begin sensing that visual information.
- [06:20] Now your retinas dont receive a full image like a movie being projected onto a screen.
- [06:24] Its more like a bunch of pixel points of light energy that millions of receptors translate
- [06:29] into neural impulses and zip back into the brain.
- [06:32] These retinal receptors are called rods and cones.
- [06:35] Our rods detect gray scale and are used in our peripheral vision as well as to avoid
- [06:39] stubbing our toes in twilight conditions when we cant really see in color.
- [06:42] Our cones detect fine detail and color.
- [06:45] Concentrated near the retinas central focal point called the fovea, cones function only
- [06:49] in well lit conditions, allowing you to appreciate the intricacies of your grandmas china
- [06:54] pattern or your uncles sleeve tattoo.
- [06:56] And the human eye is terrific at seeing color.
- [07:00] Our difference threshold for colors is so exceptional that the average person can distinguish
- [07:04] a million different hues.
- [07:06] Theres a good deal of ongoing research around exactly how our color vision works.
- [07:10] But two theories help us explain some of what we know.
- [07:12] One model, called the Young-Helmholtz trichromatic theory suggests that the retina houses three
- [07:17] specific color receptor cones that register red, green and blue, and when stimulated together,
- [07:22] their combined power allows the eye to register any color.
- [07:25] Unless, of course youre colorblind.
- [07:27] About one in fifty people have some level of color vision deficiency.
- [07:30] Theyre mostly dudes because the genetic defect is sex linked.
- [07:33] If you cant see the Crash Course logo pop out at you in this figure, its likely that
- [07:36] your red or green cones are missing or malfunctioning which means you have dichromatic instead of
- [07:41] trichromatic vision and cant distinguish between shades of red and green.
- [07:45] The other model for color vision, known as the opponent-process theory, suggests that
- [07:49] we see color through processes that actually work against each other.
- [07:52] So some receptor cells might be stimulated by red but inhibited by green, while others
- [07:57] do the opposite, and those combinations allow us to register colors.
- [08:01] But back to your eyeballs.
- [08:02] When stimulated, the rods and cones trigger chemical changes that spark neural signals
- [08:06] which in turn activate the cells behind them called bipolar cells, whose job it is to turn
- [08:11] on the neighboring ganglion cells.
- [08:14] The long axon tails of these ganglions braid together to form the ropy optic nerve, which
- [08:19] is what carries the neural impulses from the eyeball to the brain.
- [08:22] That visual information then slips through a chain of progressively complex levels as
- [08:26] it travels from optic nerve, to the thalamus, and on to the brains visual cortex.
- [08:30] The visual cortex sits at the back of the brain in the occipital lobe, where the right
- [08:34] cortex processes input from the left eye and vice versa.
- [08:38] This cortex has specialized nerve cells, called feature detectors that respond to specific
- [08:43] features like shapes, angles and movements.
- [08:45] In other words different parts of your visual cortex are responsible for identifying different
- [08:50] aspects of things.
- [08:51] A person who cant recognize human faces may have no trouble picking out their set
- [08:55] of keys from a pile on the counter.
- [08:56] Thats because the brains object perception occurs in a different place from its face
- [09:00] perception.
- [09:01] In the case of Dr. Sacks, his condition affects the region of the brain called the fusiform
- [09:04] gyrus, which activates in response to seeing faces.
- [09:07] Sackss face blindness is congenital, but it may also be acquired through disease or
- [09:11] injury to that same region of the brain.
- [09:13] And some cells in a region may respond to just one type of stimulus, like posture or
- [09:17] movement or facial expression, while other clusters of cells weave all that separate
- [09:21] information together in an instant analysis of a situation.
- [09:24] That clown is frowning and running at me with a tiny cream pie.
- [09:28] Im putting these factors together.
- [09:30] Maybe I should get out of here.
- [09:31] This ability to process and analyze many separate aspects of the situation at once is called
- [09:35] parallel processing.
- [09:36] In the case of visual processing, this means that the brain simultaneously works on making
- [09:40] sense of form, depth, motion and color and this is where we enter the whole world of
- [09:45] perception which gets complicated quickly, and can even get downright philosophical.
- [09:50] So well be exploring that in depth next time but for now, if you were paying attention,
- [09:55] you learned the difference between sensation and perception, the different thresholds that
- [09:58] limit our senses, and some of the neurology and biology and psychology of human vision.
